Book Review

The Power of the Great Powers in China

— Tang Bin

Authored by Li Changfu

Superficially, our nation’s international status appears to be that of a fully independent nation. In reality, the opposite is true. We have been dominated by the imperialist powers for a long time. Anyone with a little historical knowledge understands this. According to political theory, a country that can organize its own government and rule the land and people under its jurisdiction is called an independent nation. A country that can organize a government, but where all major policies are controlled by foreigners, is called a protectorate. A country that cannot organize a government and is ruled entirely by another country is called a colony. Protectorates and colonies have incomplete sovereignty and are collectively referred to as colonies. Although our country has a self-organized government, its sovereignty cannot be fully exercised throughout the country. Therefore, although it is superficially called an independent nation, it is undoubtedly a semi-independent nation. The imperialists oppress our country and infringe upon our sovereignty. Although it cannot be said that it is a colony, it can be regarded as a semi-colony. However, according to Mr. Sun Yat-sen, it is not appropriate to call our country a semi-colony. It should be called a sub-colony. A colony is under the oppression of one imperialist power. Like our country, it is under the iron heel of international imperialism, and its status is even lower than that of a colony. This is the most accurate statement. Regarding this, Mr. Sun Yat-sen explained it very clearly in a lecture on nationalism. The main points are excerpted below:

For example, Annam is a French colony, and Korea is a Japanese colony. Annam and Korea are only slaves of one country, with only one master, with obligations, and also with rights. In our country, all treaty countries are our masters. Our country only has obligations to its masters and no rights. If Annam and Korea suffer famine, France and Japan will definitely provide relief, fulfilling their responsibilities as masters. If there is a drought in northern China, even occasional donations from foreigners are regarded as a great favor. Therefore, our country's international status is even lower than that of a colony, and it is called a sub-colony.

Of course, to understand the true nature of our country becoming a sub-colony, we must study the power of international imperialism in our country. The most significant aspect is that the great powers use force or threatening diplomatic means to force our country to conclude unequal treaties, expanding their power in our country. The power of Britain, the United States, Japan, and France is the greatest, followed by Italy, Portugal, Spain, the Netherlands, and Belgium. Other countries all have special rights to enjoy in our country. This is how hateful and painful it is. As citizens, we should pay attention to or conduct thorough research on the expansion of the powers in our country.

Statistically, our treaty countries number twenty, such as China, Japan, Britain, the United States, France, Germany, Austria, Italy, Sweden, Norway, Switzerland, Persia, Denmark, the Netherlands, Spain, Belgium, Peru, Brazil, and Portugal, Mexico, etc. Among them, the power of Japan, Britain, the United States, France, Italy, the Netherlands, Belgium, and Spain is the greatest (especially Britain, the United States, Japan, and France). Russia, Germany, and Austria had great power before the European War, but greatly declined after the war. Denmark, Portugal, and Sweden have the next level of power. As for Brazil, Peru, and Mexico, their power is even weaker. The power of various countries in our country is mostly obtained by exploiting unequal treaties. When one country obtains special rights in our country, other countries expand their power by invoking "equal opportunity" or "most-favored-nation treatment." Even countries such as Brazil, Peru, and Mexico, which have very little relationship with our country, have the right to have tariff agreements, consular jurisdiction, and dispatch warships to expand their power in our country. These are all very obvious facts.

According to Charles Hodges, an American, the power of the great powers in our country can be divided into territorial, administrative, economic, and military power. In addition to these four, the author of this book believes that a cultural aspect can be added. This is definitely true. In the introduction of the first chapter, the author lists this in a table, making it easy for us to understand at a glance. This is something that deserves our attention. The table is briefly listed below to assist researchers:

1. Territorial power: A. Ceded territory: (1) Ceded lands (e.g., Hong Kong, Taiwan); (2) Leased territories (e.g., Lushun, Weihaiwei); B. Other territory: (1) Spheres of influence; (2) Foreign railway zones; (3) Foreign settlements (i.e., concessions).

2. Administrative power: A. Transportation enterprises established by foreigners: (1) Railways (e.g., South Manchuria Railway); (2) Post offices (Japanese post offices in Manchuria not yet abolished); (3) Telegraph offices and radio stations; B. Consular jurisdiction; C. Foreign employees: (1) Foreign customs officials; (2) Foreign postal officials; (3) Foreign salt administration officials; (4) Foreign railway officials; (5) Foreign government advisors; (6) Foreign military advisors in various armies.

3. Economic power: A. Concessioned industries and commerce: (1) Private enterprises; (2) Public enterprises; B. Loans: (1) Political loans; (2) Industrial loans; C. Foreign banks.

4. Military power: A. Foreign military bases; B. Foreign guards and military police; C. Foreign warships in territorial waters and inland rivers.

5. Cultural power: A. Spread of Christianity; B. Schools established by foreigners; Newspapers and news agencies established by foreigners.

Although this table is very brief, it completely includes the power and special rights that the great powers occupy in our country. From this, it can also be understood that our country's international status is undoubtedly that of a sub-colony.

Chapter Two, "The History of the Expansion of the Powers in China," is of course very important in this book. The author uses a very concise style to describe the expansion of the powers in China, but after reading it, we can at least gain a deep understanding. Most people can understand that since the Opium War and the conclusion of the Treaty of Nanjing with Britain, foreigners have used unequal treaties to develop their power in China. Subsequently, after each war, another treaty was concluded, and international imperialism was carried out step by step. Over the past eighty years, the current situation has been created. In order to help readers understand further, the author divides the process of power expansion into five periods for description: 1. From the Opium War to the Sino-Japanese War; 2. From the Sino-Japanese War to the Boxer Rebellion; 3. From the Boxer Rebellion to the beginning of the European War; 4. From the beginning of the European War to the Washington Conference; 5. From the Washington Conference to the present. Generally speaking, the first period was the era when the powers initially occupied power; the second period was the era when the powers wantonly invaded China and attempted to divide China; the third period was the era when the powers abandoned the policy of dividing China and greatly expanded their economic power; the fourth period was the era when Japan alone expanded its power in China; the fifth period was the era when the powers abandoned individual aggression and adopted a coordinated approach, seemingly intending to jointly manage China. It is also the initial stage of the shaking of the powers. At this time, the Chinese people should be aware of this and act as a backup for the government's diplomacy, in order to eliminate the expansion of the powers in our country permanently. This meaningful movement will certainly benefit the future of our nation.

Chapter Three, "The Territorial Power of the Powers in China," divides their territorial power in China into ceded lands, leased territories, occupied territories, spheres of influence, and concessions. Regarding this, the author describes each item separately and provides detailed tables. When we look at them, anyone with a sense of national love will feel pain and indignation. Because the territory of our country occupied by Britain, Russia, Japan, and France amounts to no less than 500,000 square miles, which is one-third of the total area of ​​the country. How heartbreaking it is to read this.

Chapter Four, "The Administrative Power of the Powers in China," primarily concerns consular jurisdiction and customs administration rights, followed by railways, post offices, telegraph offices, and radio stations established by foreigners, which greatly hinder our country's sovereignty. Foreign employees hired by various agencies in our country also occupy considerable power. In this chapter, the author also uses a historical approach to describe in detail, so that our readers can obtain a systematic understanding. This is also the unique feature of this book.

Chapter Five, "The Economic Power of the Powers in China," states that the greatest power of the powers in China is actually economic power. Foreigners use administrative power to carry out their economic aggression, and its influence even tends to envelop the entire China. This is very alarming. However, in describing the economic aspect, it is easy to tend towards inaccurate theories. But the author here does not make any judgment, but objectively makes a historical description. Therefore, at the end of this chapter, he says, "The economic power of the powers in our country is driven by their political advantages. If the administrative power of foreign countries is not eliminated, our country's industry will not be able to prosper, and there is no hope for the elimination of foreign power. Therefore, to save China, the special rights of the powers in China must be abolished first." This certainly contains a full and correct truth.

Chapter Six, "The Military Power of the Powers in China," says that there are two types of foreign troops in China: one based on treaties and one not based on treaties. The former includes the troops of Britain, France, the United States, and Italy stationed along the Beijing-Fengtian Railway from Beijing to Haikou, all of which are based on the Boxer Protocol. The latter includes the troops of Japan and Russia stationed in Manchuria, the troops of Japan stationed in Qingdao and Hankou, and the troops of Britain stationed in Shanghai. According to recent surveys, there are about 20,000 foreign troops stationed in Shanghai and Beijing, and about 20,000 Japanese troops in southern Manchuria, totaling more than 40,000 foreign troops in the country. This is a very alarming number. There is no reason for foreign troops to be stationed in the territory of an independent country. The powers use the pretext of protecting overseas Chinese to station troops in our country, but in fact, they have already infringed upon our country's sovereignty. In this chapter, the author's description has the power to awaken our people's awareness.

Chapter Seven, "The Cultural Power of the Powers in China," can be divided into three categories: churches, schools, and news media. The power of this anesthetic is indeed greater than military and other aggressions, and we should pay close attention to it.

Chapter Eight is the conclusion, which summarizes the above and allows us to further understand the power of the powers in China. The most important thing, of course, is to abolish unequal treaties and eliminate their political privileges, and their economic power will naturally retreat. The author says, "Better to be a free citizen in pain than a slave in comfort in a ruined country." If we want to understand this fundamental meaning, then we must read this book. This book is based on popular simplicity and easy to understand, and it is sufficient for general reading.

British Imperialism and China

— Mo Zhi

Authored by Tang Wenqian

Anyone with a little geographical knowledge can understand that our country is located east of the East Sea, and England is located west of the West Sea. Of course, East Asia and Western Europe were originally "as different as a horse and an ox," but now they have become an incompatible force in the international arena. The facts that have occurred in between can be cited as reasons for us to cry bitterly. It is really "difficult to count on one's fingers." Our national revolution has long been successful, and now we are moving towards the great road of active construction. This is undoubtedly an excellent phenomenon. However, we must know that if we expect the strength of the country to become increasingly stronger, it undoubtedly depends on the government's efforts to develop various construction undertakings, to adopt a strong foreign policy that is beneficial to the country, and for the people to cooperate with the government and act as a strong backup for the government. In that case, the "unequal treaties," which are a major obstacle to our people's determination to become stronger, can be abolished. Because if these unequal treaties are not abolished for a day, our country's national strength will never be able to become stronger. Therefore, Premier Sun Yat-sen once said, "Unequal treaties are the Chinese people's indenture," and also said, "To ask whether foreign countries can abolish unequal treaties, we must first ask whether we have the determination to fight for it." When Premier Sun Yat-sen was dying, he still repeatedly used this as his last will, how sincere and earnest his heart was in hoping that our people would encourage our people to fight for the country. This is something everyone knows. British imperialists are the initiators of unequal treaties. This is a very deep image imprinted in the minds of the Chinese people. It is engraved in our hearts and can never be forgotten. The statement of their crimes cannot be completed in just a few words. To cite the biggest, it is from the Opium War,

Page 22

to cannon fire at Nanjing. Such a great shame and the most painful and unfortunate historical fact must arouse infinite grief and anger in any person with ambition. At the same time, if we want to study the fundamental reasons why China's national strength cannot be strong, and the causes and consequences of unequal conditions, then this book "British Imperialism and China" is worthy of our attention. At the very least, it can serve as an aid to our research on this issue.

The entire book is divided into thirteen chapters. Chapter One, "China and Britain in the Era Without Treaties," (1) "The Initial Trade Between China and Britain," (2) "The Three Missions Sent to China by the British Government," (3) "The British Warship Attacks Macao." The author first states that after Europeans discovered new sea routes at the end of the fifteenth century, the Portuguese and Spanish gradually came to China to trade. By the fourteenth year of the Jiajing era of the Ming Dynasty, the Portuguese obtained Macao as a place of residence, and the Spanish also occupied Taiwan to open markets for trade. England, which now holds the European hegemony of imperialism, was already becoming increasingly strong at this time. Therefore, it first had the intention of getting involved, and then gave birth to the heart of invading our China. Here, the author describes in chronological order based on the history of Sino-British trade, which especially allows us to understand that their ambition to invade is increasing day by day. Second, it describes the three missions sent to China by the British government. At that time, they still did not understand our country's national conditions, so they did not dare to use the threat of their warships, but hoped that China would give them equal treatment and slightly preserve the dignity of their country and the interests of their merchants. The first negotiation by the British government, from this time on, the fifth of the seven requirements they put forward was the most important, which was to obtain a piece of land in China as a base for trade, to establish a pier, and to reduce tariffs and other miscellaneous fees. Because this failed to satisfy their wishes, the attack on Macao by warships occurred. This is described in the most detail in this book, and we can also find out the true face of British imperialism from it.

Chapter Two, "The Opium War," (1) "The Cause of the War," This is of course very important. Opium has been entering China for more than 1,200 years, and the impact on our people is profound and significant. Here, the author lists detailed tables of opium import volume and silver export volume to make a comparative study. Reading it really arouses infinite sadness and hatred, which is the only good opportunity to awaken us. (2) "The State of the War," This is when Lin Zexu arrived in Guangdong, Yi Lyu sent a messenger to his country to report on the situation of China's opium ban, requesting the dispatch of warships to assist. After the British government received the report, there were those who advocated war and those who opposed war. In the end, cannon fire was exchanged. When our country failed, a peace agreement was reached, and unequal treaties were concluded as a result. (3) "The Treaty of Nanjing," These are all ropes that bind China and are the main reason why China is trapped and declining. But at that time, the British government's pretense of virtue and its ambitious aggression against our country is enough for us to hate.

Chapter Three, "The Anglo-French Allied Forces' Campaign," (1) "The Reasons for the Conflict," After the Opium War, our country's national strength declined day by day, and it was also afflicted by the change of Hong Xiuquan, the deployment of troops to raise funds, and the chaos of government orders. The British seized the opportunity to expand their trade, using treacherous merchants and giving them passports or national flags, allowing them to freely enter and exit various seaports. The treacherous merchants took advantage of the power of the British to sell opium and anesthetize our people. Later, Ba Xia Li led warships and bombarded the Huangpu Fort, and then captured Guangzhou. At that time, the Austrian people were indignant at the British tyranny. After the British army left, they competed to fight hard, destroying all the British, American, and French military buildings and foreign houses in revenge. As a result, it was not only a problem with Britain, but also promoted the Anglo-French Allied Forces' Campaign. (2) "The State of the War," The British government appointed Elgin as the plenipotentiary ambassador, and the French government appointed Luo as the plenipotentiary ambassador, leading fleets to China. Elgin first arrived in Hong Kong and wrote to Ye Mingchen (Governor of Guangdong), requesting an appointment to discuss compensation for losses and the establishment of a new treaty, otherwise they would meet with cannon fire. The French and American consuls also wrote letters requesting compensation and were willing to mediate. The result was peace talks in Tianjin. The British envoy put forward 56 articles of the proposed new treaty, and the French envoy also put forward 42 articles, forcing them to sign. The Qing court, because it had no confidence in either war or defense, signed and acknowledged without negotiation, which was the Treaty of Tianjin. It was even more severe than the Treaty of Nanjing. Regarding this treaty, the important points are excerpted by the author in the book, so that readers can understand the truth. (3) "The Disturbance Re-created," This is one wave not yet subsided, and another wave rises again. At this time, the Sino-British Beijing Treaty was also completed. The disaster of the Anglo-French Allied Forces' Campaign ended at this time, and the disasters left behind by the treaty have not yet ended. (4) "The Losses on the Treaty," This is of course shocking to us. Since the Opium War, the Treaty of Nanjing, the alliance of the city, was concluded, and our country gradually fell into the net of unequal treaties. This time, the Treaty of Tianjin was even more severe. The most important four points were: (1) the grant of consular jurisdiction; (2) the beginning of tariff rate negotiation; (3) the opening of inland rivers; (4) the beginning of the most-favored-nation treaty. This not only caused us to lose the spirit of an independent country, but also made it enough to control our country's death. The author's description here is even more detailed, and it is worth paying attention to.

Chapter Four, "The Margary Incident and the Loss of Burma," (1) "The Murder of Margary and the Yantai Treaty," (2) "The Process of Britain's Extinction of Burma," (3) "The Negotiations After the Extinction of Burma." After the conclusion of the Sino-British Treaty of Tianjin, British commerce gained another layer of protection, and the volume of trade increased year by year. Therefore, the British wanted to open up a land trade route, from India to the interior of China, and their ambitions can be seen. After Margary (the secretary of the British envoy) was killed, there was the Yantai Conference, and four points were agreed, commonly known as the Yantai Treaty, also known as the Zhifu Treaty. Needless to say, it was being invaded, making China even more depressed. Burma bordered India and was also a vassal state of our country. Britain, with its aggressive ambition, had been coveting it for a long time. When China and France had a conflict, and neither had time to take care of it, Britain used a military strategy as fast as lightning, and in less than two weeks, it swallowed it all. When our country lost this vassal state, it could only swallow its anger and say nothing. But although our country cannot argue with force, there must be negotiations on the division of boundaries and all other matters. As a result, the seeds of the Pianma negotiations also occurred at this time. In our country, not only was the barrier removed, leaving endless worries on the frontier, but also the internal rights were lost. It can be said that we are shedding tears for it. This chapter is also nothing less than a page of painful history.

Chapter Five, "The Lease of Military Ports," (1) "The Pretext for the Lease," (2) "The Lease of Weihaiwei and Kowloon." Chapter Six, "Britain in the Eight-Power Allied Forces," (1) "The Dispatch of Troops by Britain and Japan," (2) "The Waves of Indemnity," (3) "The Anglo-German Agreement and the Anglo-Japanese Alliance." Chapter Seven, "The Sino-British Tibet Issue," (1) "The Facts of Britain's Invasion of Tibet," (2) "The Tibet Negotiations at the End of the Qing Dynasty," (3) "The Tibet Negotiations at the Beginning of the Republic of China," (4) "The Dispute Over the Simla Convention," (5) "The Recent Tibet Issue." Tibet is the southwestern screen of our country, with an area of ​​about 2.5 million square miles, bordering Sichuan and Qinghai in the east, Xinjiang in the north, and British India in the northwest. Since the British annexed India, its power has spread to the south of the Himalayas, and it has long coveted our country's Tibet. Later, the British Governor in India repeatedly sent secret envoys to Tibet for exploration, but none of them could penetrate the interior. Later, when the Yantai Treaty was signed, the British took the opportunity to add another special clause, that is, the Chinese government recognized the traffic between Britain and Tibet. Of course, it had to progress step by step. Tibet, an important border region of China, became a continuation of India. Regarding this chapter, the author has fully described it, so that all our citizens can understand the importance of Tibet's border defense. This is really a feature of this book. Chapter Eight, "The Sino-British Pianma Issue," (1) "The Disputes Over the Demarcation of the Yunnan-Burma Border," (2) "The Facts of the British Seizure of Pianma." Chapter Nine, "Negotiations to Recover Weihaiwei." Chapter Ten, "The May 30th Massacre," (1) "The Truth of the Slaughter in Liuhan Shamian," (2) "The Shanghai Case Negotiations," (3) "The Hankou Case and Shamian Case Negotiations." Chapter Eleven, "The Wanzhou Massacre." Chapter Twelve, "The National Government's Negotiations with Britain," (1) "The Recovery of the Hankou and Xunyang Concessions," (2) "The British Dispatch of Troops to China," (3) "The Bombardment of Nanjing." Chapter Thirteen, "Conclusion."

After reading this book, we can deeply understand the true face of British imperialism, and how it has used various means and methods of aggression against China. Of course, it is enough to arouse our sadness and anger. The signing of the unequal Treaty of Nanjing set a precedent, and then the unequal treaties of various countries came one after another, with the intention of leading our country to destruction. But today, the anti-imperialist voice in our country is still rising, and the call to abolish unequal treaties is also very loud. This is really a sign that our country is turning from weak to strong. But these unequal treaties were first initiated by Britain. Our country was oppressed by imperialism after the Opium War, causing our country to be trapped in the net of unequal treaties of various countries. Therefore, if we want to eliminate all unequal treaties, we must properly deal with the special rights of British imperialism in China. For the present, all our people must unite as one and act as a backup for the government. All outstanding issues between China and Britain, the issues of Tibet and Pianma, should be thoroughly resolved. Regarding this, this book is described in great detail, and the author uses the position of the Three Principles of the People to dissect the relationship between British imperialism and China and its truth. This is even more worthy of our readers' recommendation.

Manuscript Guidelines for Book and Newspaper Introductions

(1) The text is limited to introducing the latest publications at home and abroad. Except for literary works, all books and newspapers on politics, economics, law, science, education, etc. are included in the introduction. However, obscene books and newspapers will not be introduced.

(2) Pictures are limited to the recent works of new domestic and foreign artists and related illustrations of the text.

(3) Texts such as textual research or research on ancient Chinese books will not be published.

(4) Pictures and photos are limited to those that have a purchase value related to the text.

(5) The maximum number of characters per manuscript is no more than 3,000.

(6) Do not write on both sides of the manuscript paper.

(7) Do not paint with oil or water on the pictures, and do not use colored paper. White paper and ink painting must be used.
